<p><i><b>Music
Box</b></i>
is
the
third
<a
href="/wiki/Studio_album?qsrc=3044"
title="Studio
album">studio
album</a>
by
American
recording
artist
<a
href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey?qsrc=3044"
title="Mariah
Carey">Mariah
Carey</a>.
It
was
released
by
<a
href="/wiki/Columbia_Records?qsrc=3044"
title="Columbia
Records">Columbia
Records</a>
on
August
31,
1993,
in
North
America.
The
album
comprises
<a
href="/wiki/Ballad?qsrc=3044"
title="Ballad">ballads</a>
primarily
co-written
by
Carey
and
<a
href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff?qsrc=3044"
title="Walter
Afanasieff">Walter
Afanasieff</a>,
with
whom
she
had
previously
worked
on
<i><a
href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Emotions
(Mariah
Carey
album)">Emotions</a></i>
(1991),
and
a
few
urban
dance
tracks.
During
the
course
of
the
album's
development,
Carey
wanted
to
broaden
her
audience,
choosing
a
more
pop-oriented
sound.
During
this
time
frame,
they
experimented
with
different
<a
href="/wiki/Organ_(music)?qsrc=3044"
title="Organ
(music)">organs</a>
and
other
musical
instruments,
leading
the
album's
sound
away
from
her
more
contemporary
previous
efforts.
Two
unused
tracks
from
the
album
sessions
were
released
as
<a
href="/wiki/A-side_and_B-side?qsrc=3044"
title="A-side
and
B-side">B-sides</a>:
&quot;Do
You
Think
of
Me&quot;
and
&quot;Everything
Fades
Away&quot;.</p>








<p>In
order
to
successfully
take
the
album
in
a
new
direction,
Carey
and
Afanasieff
sought
out
new
and
innovative
producers,
as
well
as
some
from
Carey's
previous
releases.
<a
href="/wiki/Babyface_(musician)?qsrc=3044"
title="Babyface
(musician)">Kenneth
&quot;Babyface&quot;
Edmonds</a>
first
began
working
with
Carey
on
<i>Music
Box</i>,
where
he
helped
produce
some
of
the
album's
softer
and
more
melodious
tracks,
as
well
as
being
part
of
the
songwriting
process.
Additional
writers
and
producers
were
<a
href="/wiki/Robert_Clivill%C3%A9s?qsrc=3044"
title="Robert
Clivill&eacute;s">Robert
Clivill&eacute;s</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/David_Cole_(producer)?qsrc=3044"
title="David
Cole
(producer)">David
Cole</a>
(a
pair
also
known
as
<a
href="/wiki/C%2BC_Music_Factory?qsrc=3044"
title="C+C
Music
Factory">C+C
Music
Factory</a>)
and
<a
href="/wiki/Daryl_Simmons?qsrc=3044"
title="Daryl
Simmons">Daryl
Simmons</a>.
While
the
album
featured
a
range
of
different
talented
music
producers,
the
bulk
of
the
songwriting
was
done
by
Carey
and
her
writing
partner,
Walter
Afanasieff.
In
future
projects,
they
would
continue
writing
material
for
Carey's
albums,
until
her
1999
release
<i><a
href="/wiki/Rainbow_(Mariah_Carey_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Rainbow
(Mariah
Carey
album)">Rainbow</a></i>,
where
he
is
absent
from
the
writing
credits.</p>








<p>Four
singles
were
released
from
the
album.
The
first
three,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dreamlover_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dreamlover
(song)">Dreamlover</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Hero_(Mariah_Carey_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Hero
(Mariah
Carey
song)">Hero</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Without_You_(Badfinger_song)?qsrc=3044#Mariah_Carey_version"
title="Without
You
(Badfinger
song)">Without
You</a>&quot;,
became
worldwide
chart-topping
singles,
the
later
becoming
Carey's
highest
charting
international
single
of
her
career.
&quot;Without
You&quot;
became
Carey's
first
number-one
single
in
most
European
markets
and
reached
the
top
three
in
the
United
States.
To
promote
<i>Music
Box</i>,
Carey
embarked
on
the
short
but
successful
<a
href="/wiki/Music_Box_Tour?qsrc=3044"
title="Music
Box
Tour">Music
Box
Tour</a>
which
traveled
to
select
cities
in
North
America.
Carey
was
nominated
for
<a
href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Female_Pop_Vocal_Performance?qsrc=3044"
title="Grammy
Award
for
Best
Female
Pop
Vocal
Performance">Best
Female
Pop
Vocal
Performance</a>
for
&quot;Dreamlover&quot;
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/36th_Grammy_Awards?qsrc=3044"
title="36th
Grammy
Awards">1994
Grammy
Awards</a>
and
received
the
same
nomination
for
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Hero_(Mariah_Carey_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Hero
(Mariah
Carey
song)">Hero</a>&quot;
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/37th_Grammy_Awards?qsrc=3044"
title="37th
Grammy
Awards">1995
Grammy
Awards</a>,
although
she
did
not
win
either
time.</p>








<p>After
its
release,
<i>Music
Box</i>
received
generally
mixed
reviews
from
music
critics.
The
album
faced
criticism
regarding
Carey's
more
mellow
and
lazy
tone
in
comparison
to
her
older
work.
Despite
some
unfavorable
reviews,
the
album
became
a
commercial
success,
topping
the
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_200?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
200"><i>Billboard</i>
200</a>
and
becoming
Carey's
first
number-one
album
in
Australia,
France,
Germany,
the
Netherlands,
Switzerland
and
the
United
Kingdom.
The
<a
href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America?qsrc=3044"
title="Recording
Industry
Association
of
America">Recording
Industry
Association
of
America</a>
(RIAA)
certified
it
<a
href="/wiki/RIAA_certification?qsrc=3044"
title="RIAA
certification">diamond</a>,
for
shipments
of
ten
million
copies
across
the
United
States.
<i>Music
Box</i>
remains
Carey's
highest
seller
and
one
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums_worldwide?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
best-selling
albums
worldwide">best-selling
albums</a>
of
all
time,
with
worldwide
sales
of
over
32
million
copies.</p>








<div
style="overflow:hidden;height:null;">

</div>

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Background">Background</span></h2>








<div
class="quotebox"
style="float:
right;
clear:
right;
margin:
0.5em
0
0.8em
1.4em;
width:
30%;
padding:
6px;
border:
1px
solid
#aaa;
font-size:
88%;
background-color:
#F9F9F9;
padding:8px">









<div
style="position:
relative;
text-align:
left;">










<div>











<p>&quot;Mariah
and
Dave
did
this
loop
thing,
and
it
was
new
to
us
pop
producers
at
the
time.
Their
version
of
'Dreamlover'
was
missing
a
lot
of
stuff.
The
spirit
of
the
song
was
up
but
it
wasn't
hitting
hard
enough.
[My
work
on
the
track]
put
a
whole
different
shade
of
colors
into
it.&quot;</p>










</div>









</div>









<div
style="text-align:
left;">









—Afanasieff
on
&quot;Dreamlover&quot;
before
and
after
he
worked
on
it.









<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>








</div>








</div>








<p>In
1988,
Carey
was
discovered
by
<a
href="/wiki/Tommy_Mottola?qsrc=3044"
title="Tommy
Mottola">Tommy
Mottola</a>,
CEO
of
<a
href="/wiki/Columbia_Records?qsrc=3044"
title="Columbia
Records">Columbia
Records</a>,
and
was
promptly
signed
to
the
label.
Carey's
<a
href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Mariah
Carey
(album)">self-titled</a>
debut
<a
href="/wiki/Studio_album?qsrc=3044"
title="Studio
album">studio
album</a>,
released
the
following
year,
focused
on
re-recording
and
mastering
several
songs
she
had
already
written
in
high
school
alongside
classmate,
<a
href="/wiki/Ben_Margulies?qsrc=3044"
title="Ben
Margulies">Ben
Margulies</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson_1-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson-1">[2]</a></sup>
Aside
from
the
seven
songs
taken
from
Carey's
demo
tape,
four
other
tracks
were
written
and
produced
by
the
former
and
an
array
of
famed
record
producers.
The
album
was
complimented
by
critics,
who
called
it
a
mature
debut,
full
of
various
genre
influences
ranging
from
<a
href="/wiki/Pop_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Pop
music">pop</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B?qsrc=3044"
title="Contemporary
R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Soul_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Soul
music">soul</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson2_2-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson2-2">[3]</a></sup>
The
album
became
a
commercial
success,
selling
over
fifteen
million
units
globally.<sup
id="cite_ref-bet_3-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bet-3">[4]</a></sup>
While
making
a
strong
impact
on
pop
music,
Carey
became
interested
in
altering
her
sound,
and
deviating
from
pop
music
for
her
second
studio
effort,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Emotions
(Mariah
Carey
album)">Emotions</a></i>
(1991).<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson2_2-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson2-2">[3]</a></sup>
Following
the
success
of
her
debut,
Columbia
allowed
her
to
take
more
control
over
her
musical
departure,
enabling
her
to
change
her
genre
infusions,
melodies
and
production.
During
the
album's
recording,
Carey
worked
with
several
different
musicians
and
producers,
aside
from
<a
href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff?qsrc=3044"
title="Walter
Afanasieff">Walter
Afanasieff</a>,
the
only
hold
over
from
her
debut.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson3_4-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson3-4">[5]</a></sup></p>








<p><i>Emotions</i>
contained
influences
from
mostly
1950s,
1960s
and
1970s
balladry
and
<a
href="/wiki/Gospel_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Gospel
music">gospel</a>,
as
well
as
her
continued
work
of
R&amp;B
and
soul.
The
album,
while
praised
by
some
as
more
mature
and
raw,
failed
to
reach
the
critical
or
commercial
heights
of
her
debut
effort,
while
selling
far
less
and
failed
to
introduce
Carey
into
a
different
market.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson4_5-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson4-5">[6]</a></sup>
Following
these
events,
Columbia
decided
to
try
to
market
Carey
in
a
similar
fashion
to
her
debut,
only
having
her
produce
a
more
commercial
and
radio-friendly
album.
Their
plans
were
to
tone
down
Carey's
vocals,
and
soften
the
album's
production,
leaving
a
more
contemporary
pop/R&amp;B
record.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson5_6-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson5-6">[7]</a></sup>
Agreeing
to
the
change,
Carey
and
Afanasieff
began
writing
and
recording
material
for
her
third
studio
effort,
<i>Music
Box</i>.
On
the
album's
first
track
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dreamlover_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dreamlover
(song)">Dreamlover</a>&quot;,
Carey
worked
with
<a
href="/wiki/Dave_Hall_(record_producer)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dave
Hall
(record
producer)">Dave
Hall</a>
throughout
the
song's
entire
production.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-nickson6_7-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson6-7">[8]</a></sup>
In
order
to
help
with
some
of
the
song's
arrangements,
Mottola
enrolled
the
help
of
<a
href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff?qsrc=3044"
title="Walter
Afanasieff">Walter
Afanasieff</a>,
who
took
on
the
completed
track
and
transformed
it
into
a
more
commercial
hit.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup></p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Writing_and_development">Writing
and
development</span></h2>








<p>With
Carey
in
the
captain's
chair,
having
more
control
than
she
had
on
any
other
album,
she
took
the
album
in
a
new
direction,
alongside
Afanasieff.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
For
Carey's
third
studio
effort,
she
enrolled
the
help
of
a
range
of
songwriters,
as
well
as
record
producers.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
Aside
from
Afanasieff,
<a
href="/wiki/Babyface_(musician)?qsrc=3044"
title="Babyface
(musician)">Kenneth
&quot;Babyface&quot;
Edmonds</a>,
a
man
who
would
collaborate
with
Carey
often
in
the
near
future,
also
took
part
in
the
project.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
Babyface,
who
helped
produce
much
of
the
album,
also
co-wrote
a
track
with
Carey
titled
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Never_Forget_You?qsrc=3044"
title="Never
Forget
You">Never
Forget
You</a>&quot;,
a
song
that
was
released
as
a
B-side
to
&quot;Without
You&quot;
exclusively
in
the
United
States.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
The
album,
which
consisted
mostly
of
slower
ballads
(with
the
exception
of
&quot;Dreamlover&quot;
and
&quot;Now
That
I
Know&quot;),
contained
collaborations
with
some
carry-on
producers
and
writers
from
<i><a
href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Emotions
(Mariah
Carey
album)">Emotions</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
Of
these
were
<a
href="/wiki/C%2BC_Music_Factory?qsrc=3044"
title="C+C
Music
Factory">Clivill&eacute;s
&amp;
Cole</a>
(of
C+C
Music
Factory),
who
co-wrote
the
track
&quot;Now
That
I
Know&quot;,
a
danceable
pop
ballad,
which
used
similar
formulas
and
synthesizers
from
tracks
on
<i>Emotions</i>.
Another
writer–producer
that
worked
on
the
album
was
<a
href="/wiki/Dave_Hall_(record_producer)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dave
Hall
(record
producer)">David
Hall</a>,
who
with
Carey
wrote
the
U.S.
single
&quot;Dreamlover&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup></p>








<p>One
of
the
noticeable
differences
from
<i>Music
Box</i>
and
Carey's
previous
albums
was
its
sound.
The
album
was
described
by
Afanasieff
as
a
softer
and
more
pop-oriented
album,
&quot;filling
the
songs
with
air&quot;,
and
allowing
far
more
space
in
the
overall
sound.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
Another
noticeable
change
was
in
the
album's
production.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
When
<i>Mariah
Carey</i>
was
released,
critics
took
notice
of
its
&quot;overly
produced&quot;
and
&quot;studio
perfect&quot;
quality,
where
in
comparison,
<i>Emotions</i>
maintained
a
&quot;raw,
live
sound.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-11"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
<i>Music
Box</i>
however,
fell
in
between
the
two,
a
decision
made
by
Carey
during
the
album's
production.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-12"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
She
would
layer
each
track
with
live
backing
vocals,
so
not
to
sound
too
overly
produced,
but
still
kept
the
inclusion
of
musical
synthesizers.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-13"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup></p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>








<table
class="metadata
mbox-small"
style="border:1px
solid
#aaa;
background-color:#f9f9f9;">









<tbody>









<tr>











<td
class="mbox-image">












<div
class="center">













<div
class="floatnone">













<img
alt=""
src="http://rpmedia.ask.com:80/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png"
width="50"
height="50"
/>












</div>












</div>
</td>











<td
class="mbox-text
plainlist"
style="line-height:1.1em;">












<div
class="haudio">













<div
style="padding:2px
0;"
class="fn">













<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_-_Hero.ogg"
title="File:Mariah
Carey
-
Hero.ogg">&quot;Hero&quot;</a>












</div>













<div>













<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_-_Hero.ogg"
title="File:Mariah
Carey
-
Hero.ogg">noicon</a>












</div>













<div
style="padding:2px
0
0
0;"
class="description">













One
of
Carey's
most
inspirational
ballads,
becoming
a
chart
success
in
many
countries
around
the
world.












</div>












</div>

/>












<div
class="haudio">

style="padding:2px

<a

title="File:Mariah

-

You
Need
a
Friend&quot;</a>












</div>













<div>













<a

title="File:Mariah

-

The
final
single
from
the
album.
Was
described
by
critics
as
the
one
of

only
songs
on
the
album
to
feature

of

<td
colspan="2"
class="mbox-text"
style="line-height:1.1em;"><span
style="font-size:smaller;"><i>Problems
listening
to

<a

<p>According
to
Marc
Shapiro,
<i>Music
Box</i>
reflected
signs
of
Carey's
vocal
maturity,
as
well
as
representing
an
album
she
was
truly
proud
of.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
The
album's
first
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dreamlover_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dreamlover
(song)">Dreamlover</a>&quot;
was
described
as
a
&quot;slight
piece
of
pop
fluff,&quot;
representing
a
more
commercial
side
to
Carey
than
the
&quot;more
ambitious,&quot;
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Vision_of_Love?qsrc=3044"
title="Vision
of
Love">Vision
of
Love</a>&quot;.
Critics
believed
the
song's
chart
performance
was
due
to
its
summer
release,
as
people
were
still
looking
for
a
&quot;not-too-heavy&quot;
and
more
diverse
sound.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
The
song's
composition
was
described
as
&quot;mid
<a
href="/wiki/Tempo?qsrc=3044"
title="Tempo">tempo</a>
and
mildly
dance-able,&quot;
with
Carey's
voice
being
called
&quot;perpetually
happy,&quot;
like
a
&quot;little-girl
voice.&quot;
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Hero_(Mariah_Carey_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Hero
(Mariah
Carey
song)">Hero</a>&quot;,
the
album's
second
single,
was
one
of
Carey's
most
inspirational
ballads
at
the
time.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-14"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup></p>








<div
class="quotebox"
style="float:
left;
clear:
left;
margin:
0.5em
1.4em
0.8em
0;
width:
294px;
padding:
6px;
border:
1px
solid
#aaa;
font-size:
88%;
background-color:
#F9F9F9;">









<div
style="position:
relative;
text-align:
left;">










<div>











<p>&quot;Its
not
that
I'm
experimenting
with
lower
notes.
I
actually
think
my
natural
voice
is
low.
My
speaking
voice
is
low,
you
know
what
I
mean?
And
I'm
really
comfortable
singing
in
my
lower
register...,
I
sing
from
the
heart.
Whatever
the
music
makes
me
feel
at
the
time,
I
go
into
the
studio
to
sing
a
song,
that's
what
it's
going
to
do.
Some
people
like
it,
some
people
don't.
But
it's
just
a
part
of
my
voice
and
that's
it.&quot;</p>










</div>









</div>









<div
style="text-align:
left;">









—Carey,
on
her
vocals
on










<i>Music
Box</i>,
as
well
as
her
voice
in
general









<sup
id="cite_ref-nick5_9-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick5-9">[10]</a></sup>








</div>








</div>








<p>The
song
was
described
as
&quot;a
lush
ballad,&quot;
with
Carey
making
use
of
her
impressive,
&quot;lower
<a
href="/wiki/Alto?qsrc=3044"
title="Alto">alto</a>
register.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
As
one
of
the
more
emotional
tracks
on
the
album,
&quot;Hero&quot;
built
emotion,
verse
through
verse,
where
the
lyrics
and
melody
finally
&quot;broke
through.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Anytime_You_Need_a_Friend?qsrc=3044"
title="Anytime
You
Need
a
Friend">Anytime
You
Need
a
Friend</a>&quot;,
was
another
pop
ballad
in
which
Carey
would,
&quot;let
her
voice
roam
free,&quot;
a
feature
critics
felt
lacked
on
the
album.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
The
song
featured
&quot;rough
and
low
vocals,&quot;,
as
well
as
some
glimpses
of
Carey's
upper
<a
href="/wiki/Vocal_register?qsrc=3044"
title="Vocal
register">registers</a>.
As
with
most
of
the
songs
on
<i>Music
Box</i>,
the
lyrics
boasted
a
positive
message,
and
was
the
only
song
on
the
album
to
feature
traces
of
<a
href="/wiki/Gospel_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Gospel
music">gospel-inspired
vocals</a>
throughout
the
chorus.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup></p>








<p>The
album's
title
track,
<i>Music
Box</i>,
was
another
ballad
Carey
wrote
with
Afanasieff.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
The
song
was
described
as
one
of
Carey's
more
difficult
<a
href="/wiki/Musical_composition?qsrc=3044"
title="Musical
composition">compositions</a>,
due
to
its
&quot;softness.&quot;
The
song
requires
a
great
deal
of
<a
href="/wiki/Legato?qsrc=3044"
title="Legato">legato</a>,
to
keep
&quot;the
tunes
softness
and
sweetness,
without
resorting
to
volume.&quot;
Carey's
vocals
on
the
track
are
defined
as
&quot;soft
and
controlled,&quot;
managing
to
maintain
the
delicate
balance
in
a
manner
that
seems
effortless,
floating
easily
over
the
keyboard
and
the
shimmer
of
the
guitar.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
Lyrically,
due
to
the
song's
message
of
&quot;commitment
and
promise,&quot;
and
the
&quot;tinkling
music-box
line
played
on
the
synthesizer,&quot;
the
track
gives
the
sensation
of
a
wedding
vow
recital.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
&quot;Never
Forget
You,&quot;
one
of
the
album's
B-sides,
is
a
slow
song,
further
connecting
it
to
the
song's
message
of
&quot;lamenting
the
loss
of
love,
in
a
very
tender
way.&quot;
The
song
contains
lovely
keyboard
notes
that
hover
over
the
verses
and
allow
Carey
to
indulge
in
her
backing
vocals.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
It
was
described
as
a
&quot;stand
out
track,&quot;
one
that
could
have
easily
become
a
hit
single,
&quot;with
an
appeal
that
would
have
easily
transcended
generational
barriers.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup></p>

<p>Upon
release,
the
album
received
mixed
reviews
from
critics,
with
positive
attention
given
to
its
lyrics.
The
album's
energy
level
and
Carey's
vocal
style,
however,
were
almost
unanimously
criticized.
Critics
felt
Carey
became
&quot;too
mellow&quot;
and
&quot;somewhat
lazy&quot;
on
the
album,
especially
in
comparison
to
her
previous
work,
with
Ron
Wynn
writing,
&quot;sometimes
excessive
spirit
is
preferable
to
an
absence
of
passion&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-about_11-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-about-11">[12]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-allmusic_12-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-allmusic-12">[13]</a></sup>
Bill
Lamb
from
<a
href="/wiki/About.com?qsrc=3044"
title="About.com">About.com</a>
gave
the
album
a
generally
positive
review,
calling
it
&quot;another
good
collection
of
dance
music
and
ballads&quot;
and
writing
that
&quot;the
songwriting
is
generally
high
quality,
and
Nilsson's
'Without
You'
is
a
good
choice
for
a
cover&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-about_11-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-about-11">[12]</a></sup>
On
a
more
critical
note,
however,
Lamb
described
the
album
as
a
&quot;toned
down&quot;
album,
and
that
Carey's
upper
registers
aren't
accessed
as
often
as
in
previous
releases,
writing,
&quot;Mariah
toned
down
the
vocal
gymnastics
here,
but
she
seemed
to
lose
a
lot
of
her
energy
as
well.
There
are
still
strong
songs
here,
but
the
overall
effect
is
of
a
formula
wearing
a
bit
thin.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-about_11-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-about-11">[12]</a></sup></p>








<p><a
href="/wiki/Ron_Wynn?qsrc=3044"
title="Ron
Wynn">Ron
Wynn</a>
from
<a
href="/wiki/Allmusic?qsrc=3044"
title="Allmusic">Allmusic</a>
gave
the
album
4
out
of
5
stars,
Roc
Wynn
of
Allmusic
said
that
Carey's
&quot;octave-leaping&quot;
voice
was
downplayed
in
favor
of
the
demonstration
of
&quot;her
ability
to
sing
softly
and
coolly.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-allmusic_12-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-allmusic-12">[13]</a></sup>
Although
favorably
stating
that
Carey
lowered
the
volume
on
her
vocals,
he
said
that
the
energy
had
declined,
with
the
exception
of
personality-injected
songs
like
&quot;Hero&quot;
and
&quot;Dreamlover.&quot;
Overall,
however,
he
claimed
the
album's
&quot;different&quot;
approach
was
wise.<sup
id="cite_ref-allmusic_12-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-allmusic-12">[13]</a></sup>
Ashley
S.
Battel
from
<i><a
href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
(magazine)">Billboard</a></i>
gave
the
album
a
positive
review,
writing,
&quot;While
Carey
tones
down
the
predominance
of
her
tremendous
vocal
range
throughout
much
of
this
release,
there
is
no
question
that
she
remains
the
driving
force
behind
yet
anoth[e]r
collection
of
heavy-rotation
Top-40
successes.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-bbissue_13-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bbissue-13">[14]</a></sup></p>








<p><a
href="/wiki/David_Browne?qsrc=3044"
title="David
Browne">David
Browne</a>
from
<i><a
href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly?qsrc=3044"
title="Entertainment
Weekly">Entertainment
Weekly</a></i>
gave
the
album
a
mixed
review.<sup
id="cite_ref-ew_15-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-ew-15">[16]</a></sup>
He
said
that
due
to
Carey's
lower
energy
level,
her
voice
no
longer
soars
above
the
backup
chorus,
instead
it
only
&quot;drip[s]
over
them
like
syrup
instead
of
overpowering
them;
she
lets
the
melodies
speak
for
themselves.&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-ew_15-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-ew-15">[16]</a></sup>
Browne
awarded
the
album
an
unenthusiastic
score
of
C+.<sup
id="cite_ref-ew_15-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-ew-15">[16]</a></sup>
Stephen
Holden
of
<i><a
href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone?qsrc=3044"
title="Rolling
Stone">Rolling
Stone</a></i>
complimented
Carey's
vocals,
but
found
her
lyrics
&quot;made
up
entirely
of
pop
and
soul
clich&eacute;s&quot;
and
stated
&quot;<i>Music
Box</i>
is
so
precisely
calculated
to
be
a
blockbuster
that
its
impact
is
ultimately
a
little
unnerving&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-rs_17-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-rs-17">[18]</a></sup>
However,
in
his
review
for
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times?qsrc=3044"
title="The
New
York
Times">The
New
York
Times</a></i>,
Holden
wrote
that
the
album
&quot;takes
aim
at
the
commercial
pop
mainstream
with
an
even
sharper
focus&quot;
than
Carey's
previous
albums,
while
noting
&quot;If
huge
ballads
like
'Hero,'
'Anytime
You
Need
a
Friend'
and
a
gospel-flavored
remake
of
Harry
Nilsson's
1971
hit,
'Without
You,'
traffic
in
pop
cliches,
they
are
the
top-of-the-line
in
generic
90's
pop&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-NYtimes_16-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-NYtimes-16">[17]</a></sup>
In
his
consumer
guide
for
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Village
Voice">The
Village
Voice</a></i>,
critic
<a
href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau?qsrc=3044"
title="Robert
Christgau">Robert
Christgau</a>
gave
the
album
a
&quot;dud&quot;
rating,<sup
id="cite_ref-rc_14-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-rc-14">[15]</a></sup>
indicating
&quot;a
bad
record
whose
details
rarely
merit
further
thought&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-19"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-19">[20]</a></sup>
In
a
retrospective
review,
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stone_Album_Guide?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Rolling
Stone
Album
Guide">The
New
Rolling
Stone
Album
Guide</a></i>
(2004)
noted
a
&quot;soft-rock
slump&quot;
and
stated
&quot;While
there's
nothing
wrong
with
Carey
singing
relatively
straight
for
10
songs,
there
is
a
sore
lack
of
power
here:
baleful
soaring
ballads,
pop
optimism
along
the
lines
of
smiling
through
the
tears
and
believing
in
yourself
(or
him,
or
Him),
and
hardly
a
decent
tune
in
the
lot&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-rsguide_18-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-rsguide-18">[19]</a></sup></p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Commercial_performance">Commercial
performance</span></h2>








<p><i>Music
Box</i>
entered
the
US
<i><a
href="/wiki/Billboard_200?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
200">Billboard
200</a></i>
at
number
two,
with
174,000
copies
sold.<sup
id="cite_ref-20"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-20">[21]</a></sup>
In
its
fifteenth
week
after
release,
the
album
topped
the
chart
and
enjoyed
its
highest
sales
week,
selling
505,000
copies
and
staying
at
the
top
for
eight
non-consecutive
weeks.<sup
id="cite_ref-21"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-21">[22]</a></sup>
It
remained
in
the
top
ten
for
thirty-one
weeks
and
on
the
<i>Billboard</i>
200
for
128
weeks
(more
than
two
years,
longer
than
any
other
of
her
albums),
re-entering
the
chart
three
times.<sup
id="cite_ref-22"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-22">[23]</a></sup>
The
album
also
reached
number
one
on
<a
href="/wiki/Top_R%26B/Hip-Hop_Albums?qsrc=3044"
title="Top
R&amp;B/Hip-Hop
Albums">Top
R&amp;B/Hip-Hop
Albums</a>.
<i>Music
Box</i>
was
the
second-best
selling
album
in
the
United
States
in
1994,
only
behind
<a
href="/wiki/Ace_of_Base?qsrc=3044"
title="Ace
of
Base">Ace
of
Base</a>'s
<i><a
href="/wiki/Happy_Nation?qsrc=3044#The_Sign"
title="Happy
Nation">The
Sign</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-23"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-23">[24]</a></sup>
As
of
2012,
the
album
has
sold
7,189,000
copies
in
the
US
according
to
Nielsen
Soundcan.<sup
id="cite_ref-24"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-24">[25]</a></sup></p>








<p>The
album
became
a
success
in
Canada,
peaking
at
number
two
on
the
charts
and
being
certified
seven-times
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_music_recording_certifications?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
music
recording
certifications">platinum</a>
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Canadian_Recording_Industry_Association?qsrc=3044"
title="Canadian
Recording
Industry
Association">Canadian
Recording
Industry
Association</a>
(CRIA).
<i>Music
Box</i>
became
Carey's
best-seller
in
Europe,
topping
the
charts
in
Australia,
Germany,
New
Zealand,
Switzerland
and
the
United
Kingdom.
In
Germany,
it
became
her
highest-charting
album,
spending
eighty
weeks
on
the
<a
href="/wiki/Media_Control_Charts?qsrc=3044"
title="Media
Control
Charts">German
Albums
Chart</a>,
eleven
of
which
were
spent
at
number
one.
Germany's
sales
of
the
album
were
also
very
impressive,
with
a
certification
of
double-platinum
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_the_Phonographic_Industry?qsrc=3044"
title="International
Federation
of
the
Phonographic
Industry">International
Federation
of
the
Phonographic
Industry</a>
(IFPI),
for
shipments
of
over
one
million
copies.<sup
id="cite_ref-mcgerman_25-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-mcgerman-25">[26]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-German_certifications_database_26-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-German_certifications_database-26">[27]</a></sup>
In
Australia,
the
album
became
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums_in_Australia?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
best-selling
albums
in
Australia">tenth
best-selling
albums
in
Australia</a>,
being
certified
twelve-times
platinum
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Australian_Recording_Industry_Association?qsrc=3044"
title="Australian
Recording
Industry
Association">Australian
Recording
Industry
Association</a>
(ARIA),
denoting
shipments
of
over
840,000
copies.<sup
id="cite_ref-kent_27-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-kent-27">[28]</a></sup>
The
album
finished
at
number
one
on
the
(ARIA)
1994
End
of
Year
Chart.<sup
id="cite_ref-28"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-28">[29]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-aus_29-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-aus-29">[30]</a></sup>
In
the
United
Kingdom,
<i>Music
Box</i>
remains
Carey's
highest
seller,
topping
the
<a
href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart?qsrc=3044"
title="UK
Singles
Chart">UK
Singles
Chart</a>
and
being
certified
five-times
platinum.
It
is
also
the
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_fastest-selling_albums_in_the_United_Kingdom?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
fastest-selling
albums
in
the
United
Kingdom">58th
fastest
selling
album
of
all
time
in
the
United
Kingdom</a>,
selling
over
200,500
copies
in
its
first
week
of
release
in
1993.
It
became
one
of
the
few
albums
to
sell
over
a
million
copies
in
France,
receiving
a
diamond
certification
and
topping
the
<a
href="/wiki/Syndicat_National_de_l%27%C3%89dition_Phonographique?qsrc=3044"
title="Syndicat
National
de
l'&Eacute;dition
Phonographique">French
Album
Charts</a>.
Sales
in
France
have
been
estimated
at
1,418,100.<sup
id="cite_ref-Les_Albums_Diamant_30-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Les_Albums_Diamant-30">[31]</a></sup>
In
Brazil,
&quot;Music
Box&quot;
remains
one
of
the
best-selling
albums
by
an
international
artist,
selling
over
800,000
copies.<sup
id="cite_ref-31"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-31">[32]</a></sup>
&quot;Music
Box&quot;
finished
number
twenty-three
on
the
all
time
album
chart
in
the
Netherlands,
where
it
was
certified
six-times
platinum.<sup
id="cite_ref-dutch_32-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-dutch-32">[33]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Dutch_Certifications_to_Music_Box_33-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Dutch_Certifications_to_Music_Box-33">[34]</a></sup></p>








<p>In
Asia,
<i>Music
Box</i>
became
one
of
the
best-selling
albums
of
1994,
selling
over
2,200,000
copies
in
Japan
alone.<sup
id="cite_ref-tvcarey_34-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tvcarey-34">[35]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Mariahnumberone_35-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Mariahnumberone-35">[36]</a></sup>
The
album's
sales
continued
to
skyrocket
in
Asia,
topping
sales
of
over
1,000,000
in
South
Korea
and
320,000
copies
in
Taiwan
as
of
1996.<sup
id="cite_ref-tvcarey_34-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tvcarey-34">[35]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-36"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-36">[37]</a></sup>
In
Malaysia,
Singapore
and
Hong
Kong,
sales
reached
110,000
and
80,000
copies
respectively.<sup
id="cite_ref-tvcarey_34-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tvcarey-34">[35]</a></sup>
In
the
United
States,
<i>Music
Box</i>
became
Carey's
best-selling
album
at
the
time,
being
certified
<a
href="/wiki/RIAA_certification?qsrc=3044"
title="RIAA
certification">diamond</a>
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America?qsrc=3044"
title="Recording
Industry
Association
of
America">RIAA</a>,
denoting
shipments
of
at
least
ten
million
copies.
According
to
<a
href="/wiki/Nielsen_SoundScan?qsrc=3044"
title="Nielsen
SoundScan">Nielsen
SoundScan</a>,
the
album
has
sold
7,189,000
copies
in
the
United
States.<sup
id="cite_ref-askbillboard_37-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-askbillboard-37">[38]</a></sup>
<i>Music
Box</i>
has
sold
over
32
million
copies
worldwide,
and
is
one
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums_worldwide?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
best-selling
albums
worldwide">best-selling
albums</a>
of
all
time.<sup
id="cite_ref-yahoo_38-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-yahoo-38">[39]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-soul_39-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-soul-39">[40]</a></sup></p>

<p>&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dreamlover_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dreamlover
(song)">Dreamlover</a>&quot;
was
released
as
the
album's
first
single
on
July
27,
1993.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro_40-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro-40">[41]</a></sup>
The
single
debuted
at
number
thirteen
on
the
<i><a
href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
(magazine)">Billboard</a></i>
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
Hot
100">Hot
100</a>,
and
due
to
its
massive
airplay,
the
song
reached
the
top
of
the
Hot
100,
staying
there
for
eight
weeks.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro_40-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro-40">[41]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Billboard_Hot_100_41-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Billboard_Hot_100-41">[42]</a></sup>
<i>Dreamlover</i>
topped
the
charts
in
Canada,
and
reached
the
top-ten
in
many
other
countries.<sup
id="cite_ref-42"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-42">[43]</a></sup>
The
song
was
certified
<a
href="/wiki/Music_recording_sales_certification?qsrc=3044"
title="Music
recording
sales
certification">platinum</a>
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America?qsrc=3044"
title="Recording
Industry
Association
of
America">Recording
Industry
Association
of
America</a>
(RIAA),
denoting
shipments
of
over
one
million
copies,
as
well
as
receiving
a
gold
certification
in
Australia
and
New
Zealand.<sup
id="cite_ref-kent_27-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-kent-27">[28]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-43"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-43">[44]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-scapolo_44-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-scapolo-44">[45]</a></sup>
The
music
video
for
&quot;Dreamlover&quot;
featured
&quot;summer
scenery,&quot;
with
scenes
of
Carey
swimming
in
a
pool
by
a
waterfall,
lying
in
a
bed
of
sunflowers,
as
well
as
singing
in
front
of
hip-hop
dancers.<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-15"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
The
video
tried
to
capture,
a
&quot;home
video&quot;
feel,
adding
to
the
song's
subtle
and
airy
nature,
something
that
only
helped
the
song
dominate
the
charts<sup
id="cite_ref-nickson7_0-16"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nickson7-0">[1]</a></sup>
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Hero_(Mariah_Carey_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Hero
(Mariah
Carey
song)">Hero</a>&quot;
served
as
the
album's
second
single,
and
was
released
on
October
19,
1993.
Music
critics
praised
the
song,
calling
it
her
most
directly
inspirational
song
since
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Make_It_Happen_(Mariah_Carey_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Make
It
Happen
(Mariah
Carey
song)">Make
It
Happen</a>&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-nick6_10-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nick6-10">[11]</a></sup>
The
song
topped
the
charts
in
the
United
States
and
the
top-five
in
many
other
major
music
markets.<sup
id="cite_ref-Billboard_Hot_100_41-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Billboard_Hot_100-41">[42]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-45"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-45">[46]</a></sup>
The
music
video
for
&quot;Hero,&quot;
featured
footage
from
Carey's
concert
at
<a
href="/wiki/Proctor%27s_Theatre_(Schenectady,_New_York)?qsrc=3044"
title="Proctor's
Theatre
(Schenectady,
New
York)">Proctor's
Theatre</a>,
as
was
done
for
Carey's
following
music
video
for
&quot;Without
You.&quot;</p>








<p>&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Without_You_(Badfinger_song)?qsrc=3044#Mariah_Carey_version"
title="Without
You
(Badfinger
song)">Without
You</a>&quot;,
the
album's
third
single,
became
the
biggest
international
hit
of
Carey's
career.
&quot;Without
You&quot;
reached
number
three
in
the
United
States,
but
experienced
its
real
success
throughout
Europe.<sup
id="cite_ref-Billboard_Hot_100_41-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Billboard_Hot_100-41">[42]</a></sup>
The
song
became
Carey's
first
number
one
chart
topper
in
France,
Germany,
Sweden,
Switzerland
and
the
United
Kingdom,
propelling
the
sales
of
<i>Music
Box</i>
throughout
the
world.<sup
id="cite_ref-46"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-46">[47]</a></sup>
Carey
brought
the
song
renewed
popularity,
through
her
highly
successful
adaptation.
It
became
on
of
the
&quot;fifty
most-played
songs
of
the
year&quot;
and
even
outsold
<a
href="/wiki/Harry_Nilsson?qsrc=3044"
title="Harry
Nilsson">Harry
Nilsson</a>'s
version,
receiving
many
awards
and
recognition.<sup
id="cite_ref-47"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-47">[48]</a></sup>
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Anytime_You_Need_a_Friend?qsrc=3044"
title="Anytime
You
Need
a
Friend">Anytime
You
Need
a
Friend</a>&quot;,
the
album's
final
single,
experienced
success
on
the
charts,
reaching
the
top-ten
in
many
countries,
as
well
as
peaking
at
number
twelve
in
the
United
States.<sup
id="cite_ref-Billboard_Hot_100_41-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Billboard_Hot_100-41">[42]</a></sup>
It
became
the
second
single
in
<i>Music
Box</i>
to
perform
better
throughout
Europe
than
in
the
U.S.<sup
id="cite_ref-Mccharts2_48-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Mccharts2-48">[49]</a></sup></p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Promotion">Promotion</span></h2>








<div
class="rellink
relarticle
mainarticle">








Main
article:









<a
href="/wiki/Music_Box_Tour?qsrc=3044"
title="Music
Box
Tour">Music
Box
Tour</a>







</div>








<div
class="thumb
tright">









<div
class="thumbinner"
style="width:327px;">









<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_Neighborhood_Ball_in_downtown_Washington_2009_2.JPG"
class="image"><img
alt=""
src="http://rpmedia.ask.com:80/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Mariah_Carey_Neighborhood_Ball_in_downtown_Washington_2009_2.JPG/325px-Mariah_Carey_Neighborhood_Ball_in_downtown_Washington_2009_2.JPG"
width="325"
height="216"
class="thumbimage"
/></a>










<div
class="thumbcaption">











<div
class="magnify">











<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_Neighborhood_Ball_in_downtown_Washington_2009_2.JPG"
class="internal"
title="Enlarge"><img
src="/images/wikipedia/magnify-clip.png"
width="15"
height="11"
alt=""
/></a>










</div>
Carey
performing
&quot;Hero&quot;
live
during











<a
href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Barack_Obama?qsrc=3044"
title="Inauguration
of
Barack
Obama">The
Neighborhood
Inaugural
Ball</a>
for











<a
href="/wiki/Barack_Obama?qsrc=3044"
title="Barack
Obama">President
Obama</a>
in
Washington
D.C..









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>In
order
to
promote
the
album,
Carey
embarked
on
her
first-headlining
tour.
Originally,
Carey
had
not
planned
to
tour
due
to
stage
fright,
a
feat
she
followed
during
her
previous
album
releases.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
However,
after
the
continued
success
of
<i>Music
Box</i>
and
persuasion
from
<a
href="/wiki/Tommy_Mottola?qsrc=3044"
title="Tommy
Mottola">Tommy
Mottola</a>,
Carey
agreed
to
tour,
supporting
her
current
and
previous
albums.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
As
Carey
was
not
physically
or
emotionally
ready
for
an
extensive
tour,
six
shows
were
dated,
each
with
many
days
in
between,
in
order
to
give
Carey's
voice
time
to
rest.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
An
extra
performance,
taking
place
at
<a
href="/wiki/Proctor%27s_Theatre_(Schenectady,_New_York)?qsrc=3044"
title="Proctor's
Theatre
(Schenectady,
New
York)">Proctor's
Theatre</a>
was
planned,
where
Carey
would
film
the
one-hour
special,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Here_Is_Mariah_Carey?qsrc=3044"
title="Here
Is
Mariah
Carey">Here
Is
Mariah
Carey</a></i>,
to
be
released
during
the
Christmas
season.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup></p>








<p>When
the
tickets
went
on
sales,
the
shows
did
not
sell-out
instantly;
but
were
selling
at
a
healthy
pace.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
On
the
opening
night
of
Carey's
tour,
she
sang
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/Miami_Arena?qsrc=3044"
title="Miami
Arena">Miami
Arena</a>.
The
show
was
only
about
two-thirds
full,
something
that
worried
Carey's
management,
as
it
was
the
tour's
opening
night.
However,
Carey
did
not
seem
to
mind,
and
began
the
show
with
high
spirits.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
The
first
show
in
Miami
received
scathing
reviews,
with
critics
&quot;ripping
her
opening-night
performance
to
shreds.&quot;
The
following
shows
were
more
favorable
to
Carey,
not
only
were
her
succeeding
shows
sold-out,
they
also
won
rave-reviews.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
Regarding
Carey's
show
in
New
York,
<a
href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times?qsrc=3044"
title="The
New
York
Times">The
New
York
Times</a>
wrote
the
following
remarks
regarding
Carey's
voice
and
performance
on
the
tour:</p>








<blockquote>








&quot;Beyond
any
doubt,
Ms.
Carey's
voice
is
no
studio
concoction.
Her
range
extends
from
a
rich,
husky
alto
to
dog-whistle
high
notes;
she
can
linger
over
sensual
turns,
growl
with
playful
confidence,
syncopate
like
a
scat
singer.
Although
rock
concerts
aren't
known
for
precise
intonation,
she
sang
with
startlingly
exact
pitch.&quot;








<sup
id="cite_ref-nytreview_49-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nytreview-49">[50]</a></sup>







</blockquote>








<p>Aside
from
touring
for
the
first
time
in
her
career,
Carey
visited
various
American
and
European
television
programs,
where
she
performed
different
singles
from
the
album.
In
late
1993,
Carey
appeared
on
<a
href="/wiki/The_Arsenio_Hall_Show?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Arsenio
Hall
Show">The
Arsenio
Hall
Show</a>,
where
she
performed
both
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dreamlover_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dreamlover
(song)">Dreamlover</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Hero_(Mariah_Carey_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Hero
(Mariah
Carey
song)">Hero</a>.&quot;
Other
performances
Carey
staged
during
1993
included,
&quot;Hero&quot;
on
<a
href="/wiki/The_Jay_Leno_Show?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Jay
Leno
Show">The
Jay
Leno
Show</a>
and
&quot;Dreamlover&quot;
on
her
first
visit
to
<a
href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops?qsrc=3044"
title="Top
of
the
Pops">Top
of
the
Pops</a>.
Throughout
1994,
Carey
continued
the
album's
promotion,
performing
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Without_You_(Badfinger_song)?qsrc=3044#Mariah_Carey_version"
title="Without
You
(Badfinger
song)">Without
You</a>&quot;
on
a
second
visit
to
&quot;Top
of
the
Pops,&quot;
as
well
as
visiting
France,
Germany,
Japan,
Spain
and
Sweden.
In
the
midst
of
her
&quot;Music
Box
Tour,&quot;
Carey
had
already
began
working
with
Walter
Afanasieff
on
her
holiday
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Merry_Christmas_(Mariah_Carey_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Merry
Christmas
(Mariah
Carey
album)">Merry
Christmas</a></i>,
which
was
to
be
released
during
the
Christmas
season
of
the
following
year.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup>
Additionally,
Carey
and
Affanasieff
reportedly
already
began
experimenting
with
different
ideas
and
musical
components
for
her
1995
studio
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Daydream_(Mariah_Carey_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Daydream
(Mariah
Carey
album)">Daydream</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-shapiro3_8-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shapiro3-8">[9]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Album_credits">Album
credits</span></h2>








<table
class="multicol"
style="border-collapse:
collapse;
padding:
0px;
border:
0px;
background:transparent;
width:100%;">









<tbody>









<tr>











<td
style="width:50%;
text-align:left;
vertical-align:top;">












<dl>













<dt>













Personnel













<sup
id="cite_ref-Booklet_50-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Booklet-50">[51]</a></sup>












</dt>












</dl>












<ul>













<li>Mariah
Carey
–
lead
vocals,
background
vocals</li>













<li><a
href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff?qsrc=3044"
title="Walter
Afanasieff">Walter
Afanasieff</a>
–
keyboards,
additional
keyboards,
synthesizers</li>













<li>Dave
Hall
–
synthesizers,
keyboards,
rhythm
programming</li>

<script
type="text/javascript">

(function()
{




$(document).ready(function(){
	
	
		$(".preview").click(function(e){	




		window.location
=
$(this).attr('value');	








});
		




	$(".preview").hover(function(e){	




		$('#preview').empty();




		$("#preview").append("<img
id='previewImg'
src='"+$(this).find('img').attr('src')+"'
alt='Smiley
face'
"+$(this).find('img').attr('value')+"='130'/>");	




		$("#preview")
				.css("display","block")




			.css("top",$(this).position().top
+
"px")




			.css("left",$(this).position().left
+
"px")
				.css("z-index","0")




			.fadeIn("fast");
			
			$("#previewAnc")
				.css("display","block")
				.css("top",($(this).position().top
-
1)
+
"px")




			.css("left",($(this).position().left
-
1)
+
"px")
				.css("z-index","1")




			.fadeIn("fast")
				.attr('onmousedown',
$(this).attr('onmousedown'))




			.attr('href',
$(this).attr('value'));








});
		
		$("#previewAnc").mouseout(function(){
			$('#preview')
				.css("display","none")
				.css("z-index","-1")
				.fadeIn("fast")
				.empty();
			$(this).css("display","none").css("z-index","-1");
		});
	
	});
})();

</script>

function
addEvent(obj,
evType,
fn)

{
	if(obj.addEventListener)

	{

		obj.addEventListener(evType,
fn,false);

		return
true;

	}

	else
if
(obj.attachEvent)

	{

		var
r
=
obj.attachEvent("on"+evType,
fn);

		return
r;

	}
	else
	{

		return
false;

	}

}
